Another type of project entrepreneurship involves entrepreneurs working with business students to get analytical work done on their ideas.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Social">Social</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Entrepreneurship&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Social"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Tulane_University_Environmental_Action_League,_New_Orleans,_February_2011.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Tulane_University_Environmental_Action_League%2C_New_Orleans%2C_February_2011.jpg/250px-Tulane_University_Environmental_Action_League%2C_New_Orleans%2C_February_2011.jpg" decoding="async" width="230" height="153" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Tulane_University_Environmental_Action_League%2C_New_Orleans%2C_February_2011.jpg/500px-Tulane_University_Environmental_Action_League%2C_New_Orleans%2C_February_2011.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="4256" data-file-height="2832" /></a><figcaption>Student organizers from the Green Club at Newcomb College Institute formed a social entrepreneurship organization in 2010.</figcaption></figure>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Social_entrepreneurship" title="Social entrepreneurship">Social entrepreneurship</a></div>
<p>Social entrepreneurship is the use of business techniques by <a href="/wiki/Startup_company" title="Startup company">start-up companies</a> and other entrepreneurs to develop, fund, and implement solutions to social, cultural, or environmental issues.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>111<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This concept may be applied to a variety of organizations with different sizes, aims, and beliefs.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>112<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> For-profit entrepreneurs typically measure performance using business metrics like <a href="/wiki/Profit_(economics)" title="Profit (economics)">profit</a>, <a href="/wiki/Revenues" class="mw-redirect" title="Revenues">revenues</a> and increases in expected future dividends, but social entrepreneurs are either <a href="/wiki/Non-profit_organization" class="mw-redirect" title="Non-profit organization">non-profits</a> or blend for-profit goals with generating a positive "return to society" and therefore must use different metrics. Social entrepreneurship typically attempts to further broad social, cultural, and environmental goals often associated with the <a href="/wiki/Voluntary_sector" title="Voluntary sector">voluntary sector</a><sup id="cite&#95;ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>113<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> in areas such as poverty alleviation, health care<sup id="cite&#95;ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>114<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Community_development" title="Community development">community development</a>. At times, profit-making <a href="/wiki/Social_enterprise" title="Social enterprise">social enterprises</a> may be established to support the social or cultural goals of the organization but not as an end in itself. For example, an organization that aims to provide housing and employment to the <a href="/wiki/Homelessness" title="Homelessness">homeless</a> may operate a restaurant, both to raise money and to provide employment for the homeless people.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Biosphere">Biosphere</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Entrepreneurship&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Biosphere"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Biosphere" title="Biosphere">Biosphere</a> entrepreneurship is "entrepreneurial activity that generates value for the biosphere and <a href="/wiki/Ecosystem_services" class="mw-redirect" title="Ecosystem services">ecosystem services</a>."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>115<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> It is part of a larger trend of business schools seeking to incorporate environmental topics more actively into their curricula.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>116<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Entrepreneurial_behaviours">Entrepreneurial behaviours</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Entrepreneurship&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Entrepreneurial behaviours"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Current_validated_taxonomies">Current validated taxonomies</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Entrepreneurship&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Current validated taxonomies"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Several scientifically validated frameworks have been developed to systematically classify entrepreneurial behaviors. The <a href="/wiki/Theory_of_Planned_Behavior" class="mw-redirect" title="Theory of Planned Behavior">Theory of Planned Behavior</a> (TPB), developed by <a href="/wiki/Icek_Ajzen" title="Icek Ajzen">Icek Ajzen</a>, identifies four key behavioral components: attitude toward entrepreneurship, subjective norms, perceived behavioral control, and entrepreneurial intention, with extensive validation across over 22 countries.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>117<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>118<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Entrepreneurial_orientation" title="Entrepreneurial orientation">Entrepreneurial Orientation</a> (EO) encompasses five dimensions—innovativeness, risk-taking, proactiveness, competitive aggressiveness, and autonomy—validated through comprehensive analysis of over 62,000 citations across 822 publications.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>119<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>120<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Effectuation Theory, developed by <a href="/wiki/Saras_Sarasvathy" title="Saras Sarasvathy">Saras Sarasvathy</a>, provides a taxonomy of decision-making behaviors with five principles (Bird-in-Hand, Affordable Loss, Crazy Quilt, Lemonade, and Pilot-in-the-Plane) validated through longitudinal studies of expert entrepreneurs and generating over 6,800 citations.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>121<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>122<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Emerging frameworks include the Twelve Pillars of Entrepreneurship, developed by Dane Wagner and Dr. Nikki Blacksmith at Symeta Behavior Science, which organizes behaviors into four cornerstones (Cognition, Action, Relational, and Motivational) encompassing twelve dimensions of behaviors: Vision, Strategy, Resourcefulness, Collaboration, Direction, Influence, Decision Making, Innovation, Execution, Autonomy, Intensity, and Tenacity.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>123<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This framework has a foundation in meta-analysis of over 1,000 studies, and well documented use within incubators and investment firms. The Individual Entrepreneurial Orientation (IEO) scale, developed by Clark, Covin, and Pidduck (2024), represents another advancement in measuring individual-level entrepreneurial behaviors through a validated 17-item instrument that addresses the gap between firm-level and personal behavioral measurement.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>124<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> These taxonomies collectively provide comprehensive frameworks for understanding, measuring, and predicting entrepreneurial behaviors across different contexts and levels of analysis.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Uncertainty_perception_and_risk-taking">Uncertainty perception and risk-taking</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Entrepreneurship&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Uncertainty perception and risk-taking"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Theorists <a href="/wiki/Frank_Knight" title="Frank Knight">Frank Knight</a><sup id="cite&#95;ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>125<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Peter_Drucker" title="Peter Drucker">Peter Drucker</a> defined entrepreneurship in terms of risk-taking. The entrepreneur is willing to put his or her career and financial security on the line and take risks in the name of an idea, spending time as well as capital on an uncertain venture. However, entrepreneurs often do not believe that they have taken an enormous amount of risks because they do not perceive the level of uncertainty to be as high as other people do. Knight classified three types of uncertainty:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Risk" title="Risk">Risk</a>, which is measurable statistically (such as the probability of drawing a red color ball from a jar containing five red balls and five white balls)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ambiguity" title="Ambiguity">Ambiguity</a>, which is hard to measure statistically (such as the probability of drawing a red ball from a jar containing five red balls but an unknown number of white balls)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Uncertainty" title="Uncertainty">True uncertainty</a> or Knightian uncertainty, which is impossible to estimate or predict statistically (such as the probability of drawing a red ball from a jar whose contents, in terms of numbers of coloured balls, are entirely unknown)</li></ul>
<p>Entrepreneurship is often associated with true uncertainty, particularly when it involves the creation of a novel good or service, for a market that did not previously exist, rather than when a venture creates an incremental improvement to an existing product or service. A 2014 study at ETH Zürich found that compared with typical managers, entrepreneurs showed higher decision-making efficiency and a stronger activation in regions of frontopolar cortex (FPC) previously associated with explorative choice.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>126<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Designing_individual/opportunity_nexus"><span id="Designing_individual.2Fopportunity_nexus"></span>Designing individual/opportunity nexus</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Entrepreneurship&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Designing individual/opportunity nexus"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>According to Shane and Venkataraman, entrepreneurship comprises both "enterprising individuals" and "entrepreneurial opportunities", so researchers should study the nature of the individuals who identify opportunities when others do not, the opportunities themselves and the nexus between individuals and opportunities.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>127<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> On the other hand, Reynolds et al.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>128<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> argue that individuals are motivated to engage in entrepreneurial endeavours driven mainly by necessity or opportunity, that is individuals pursue entrepreneurship primarily owing to survival needs, or because they identify business opportunities that satisfy their need for achievement. For example, higher <a href="/wiki/Economic_inequality" title="Economic inequality">economic inequality</a> tends to increase necessity-based entrepreneurship rates at the individual level.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>129<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Opportunity_perception_and_biases">Opportunity perception and biases</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Entrepreneurship&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Opportunity perception and biases"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>One study has found that certain genes affecting personality may influence the income of self-employed people.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>130<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Some people may be able to use<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Avoid_weasel_words" class="mw-redirect" title="Wikipedia:Avoid weasel words"><span title="Who says this? (February 2015)">weasel&#160;words</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> "an innate ability" or <a href="/wiki/Spiral_of_silence#Quasi-statistical_organ" title="Spiral of silence">quasi-statistical sense</a> to gauge <a href="/wiki/Public_opinion" title="Public opinion">public opinion</a><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Miller&#95;2005:278&#95;131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Miller_2005:278-131"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>131<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and market demand for new products or services. Entrepreneurs tend to have the ability to see unmet market needs and underserved markets. While some entrepreneurs assume they can sense and figure out what others are thinking, the mass media plays a crucial role in shaping views and demand.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEScheufeleMoy2000&#95;132-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEScheufeleMoy2000-132"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>132<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Ramoglou argues that entrepreneurs are not that distinctive and that it is essentially poor conceptualizations of "non-entrepreneurs" that maintain laudatory portraits of "entrepreneurs" as exceptional innovators or leaders<sup id="cite&#95;ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>133<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>134<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Entrepreneurs are often overconfident, exhibit illusion of control, when they are opening/expanding business or new products/services.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Zhang&#95;Cueto&#95;Study&#95;of&#95;Bias&#95;in&#95;Entrepreneurship&#95;27-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zhang_Cueto_Study_of_Bias_in_Entrepreneurship-27"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>27<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Styles">Styles</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Entrepreneurship&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Styles"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Differences in entrepreneurial organizations often partially reflect their founders' <a href="/wiki/Heterogenous" class="mw-redirect" title="Heterogenous">heterogenous</a> identities. Fauchart and Gruber have classified entrepreneurs into three main types: <a href="/wiki/Charles_Darwin" title="Charles Darwin">Darwinians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Communitarianism" title="Communitarianism">communitarians</a> and <a href="/wiki/Missionary" title="Missionary">missionaries</a>. These types of entrepreneurs diverge in fundamental ways in their self-views, social motivations and patterns of new firm creation.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>135<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Communication">Communication</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Entrepreneurship&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Communication"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Entrepreneurs must practice effective communication both within their firm and with external partners and investors to launch and grow a venture and enable it to survive. An entrepreneur needs a communication system that links the staff of their firm and connects the firm to outside firms and clients. Entrepreneurs should be <a href="/wiki/Charismatic_leader" class="mw-redirect" title="Charismatic leader">charismatic leaders</a>, so they can communicate a <a href="/wiki/Vision_statement" title="Vision statement">vision</a> effectively to their team and help to <a href="/wiki/Team_building" title="Team building">create a strong team</a>. Communicating a vision to followers may be the most important act of the transformational leader.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-HackmanJohnson&#95;136-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HackmanJohnson-136"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>136<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Compelling visions provide employees with a sense of purpose and encourage commitment. According to Baum et al.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Baum&#95;et&#95;al.&#95;137-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Baum_et_al.-137"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>137<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and Kouzes and Posner,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Kouzes&#95;138-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kouzes-138"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>138<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> the vision must be communicated through written statements and through in-person communication. Entrepreneurial leaders must speak and listen to articulate their vision to others.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Locke&#95;et&#95;al.&#95;139-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Locke_et_al.-139"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>139<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Communication is pivotal in the role of entrepreneurship because it enables leaders to convince potential investors, partners and employees about the feasibility of a venture.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>140<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Entrepreneurs need to communicate effectively to shareholders.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>141<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Nonverbal" class="mw-redirect" title="Nonverbal">Nonverbal</a> elements in speech such as the tone of voice, the look in the sender's eyes, body language, hand gestures and state of emotions are also important communication tools. The <a href="/wiki/Communication_Accommodation_Theory" class="mw-redirect" title="Communication Accommodation Theory">Communication Accommodation Theory</a> posits that throughout communication people will attempt to accommodate or adjust their method of speaking to others.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>142<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Face_Negotiation_Theory" class="mw-redirect" title="Face Negotiation Theory">Face Negotiation Theory</a> describes how people from different cultures manage conflict negotiation to maintain "<a href="/wiki/Face_(sociological_concept)" title="Face (sociological concept)">face</a>".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Ting&#95;Toomey&#95;143-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ting_Toomey-143"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>143<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Hugh Rank's "intensify and downplay" communications model can be used by entrepreneurs who are developing a new product or service. Rank argues that entrepreneurs need to be able to intensify the advantages of their new product or service and downplay the disadvantages to persuade others to support their venture.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>144<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Links_to_sea_piracy">Links to sea piracy</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Entrepreneurship&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Links to sea piracy"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Research from 2014 found links between entrepreneurship and historical sea <a href="/wiki/Piracy" title="Piracy">piracy</a>. In this context, the claim is made for a non-moral approach to looking at the history of piracy as a source of inspiration for entrepreneurship education<sup id="cite&#95;ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>145<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> as well as for research in entrepreneurship<sup id="cite&#95;ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>146<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and business model generation.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>147<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Psychological_makeup">Psychological makeup</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Entrepreneurship&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Psychological makeup"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Ross Levine, an economist at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_California,_Berkeley" title="University of California, Berkeley">University of California, Berkeley</a>, and Yona Rubinstein, a professor at the <a href="/wiki/London_School_of_Economics" title="London School of Economics">London School of Economics</a> released a study which suggests entrepreneurs are disproportionately white, male, from wealthy and highly educated backgrounds, and prone to "aggressive, illicit, risk-taking activities" as teenagers and young adults. Entrepreneurs also performed above average on aptitude tests.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>148<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This masculine image is also found when studying how male entrepreneurs are represented in media. A supporting but invisible family are one of the success factors when being portrayed as a male entrepreneur in media.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>149<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> A study conducted by the Census Bureau and two MIT professors, after compiling a list of 2.7&#160;million company founders who hired at least one employee between 2007 and 2014, found the average age of a successful start-up founder when he or she founded it is 45. They consistently found chances of entrepreneurial success rises with age.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>150<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>151<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Stanford_University" title="Stanford University">Stanford University</a> economist <a href="/wiki/Edward_Lazear" title="Edward Lazear">Edward Lazear</a> found in a 2005 study that variety in education and in <a href="/wiki/Work_experience" class="mw-redirect" title="Work experience">work experience</a> was the most important trait that distinguished entrepreneurs from non-entrepreneurs<sup id="cite&#95;ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>152<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> A 2013 study by Uschi Backes-Gellner of the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Zurich" title="University of Zurich">University of Zurich</a> and Petra Moog of the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Siegen" title="University of Siegen">University of Siegen</a> in Germany found that a diverse social network was also an important characteristic of students that would go on to become entrepreneurs.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>153<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>154<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Studies show that the psychological propensities for male and <a href="/wiki/Female_entrepreneur" class="mw-redirect" title="Female entrepreneur">female entrepreneurs</a> are more similar than different. <a href="/wiki/Empirical_studies" class="mw-redirect" title="Empirical studies">Empirical studies</a> suggest that female entrepreneurs possess strong negotiating skills and consensus-forming abilities.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Entrepreneurship&#95;155-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Entrepreneurship-155"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>155<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Åsa Hansson, who looked at empirical evidence from Sweden, found that the probability of becoming self-employed decreases with age for women, but increases with age for men.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:0&#95;156-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-156"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>156<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> She also found that marriage increased the probability of a person's becoming an entrepreneur.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:0&#95;156-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-156"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>156<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Jesper Sørensen wrote in 2010 that significant influences on the decision to become an entrepreneur include workplace peers and social composition. Sørensen discovered a correlation between working with former entrepreneurs and how often these individuals become entrepreneurs themselves, compared to those who did not work with entrepreneurs.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Sorensen&#95;157-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sorensen-157"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>157<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Social composition can influence entrepreneurialism in peers by demonstrating the possibility for success, stimulating a "He can do it, why can't I?" attitude. As Sørensen stated: "When you meet others who have gone out on their own, it doesn't seem that crazy."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-sorensen&#95;interview&#95;158-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sorensen_interview-158"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>158<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Entrepreneurs may also be driven to entrepreneurship by past experiences. If someone has faced multiple work stoppages or has been unemployed in the past, the probability of becoming an entrepreneur increases<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:0&#95;156-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-156"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>156<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Per Cattell's personality framework, both personality traits and attitudes are thoroughly investigated by psychologists. However, in case of entrepreneurship research these notions are employed by academics<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Avoid_weasel_words" class="mw-redirect" title="Wikipedia:Avoid weasel words"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too vague attribution or weasel words. (March 2020)">which?</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> too, but vaguely. Cattell states that personality is a system that is related to the environment and further adds that the system seeks explanation to the complex transactions conducted by both—traits and attitudes. This is because both of them bring about change and growth in a person. Personality is that which informs what an individual will do when faced with a given situation. A person's response is triggered by his/her personality and the situation that is faced.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>159<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Innovative entrepreneurs may be more likely to experience what psychologist <a href="/wiki/Mihaly_Csikszentmihalyi" title="Mihaly Csikszentmihalyi">Mihaly Csikszentmihalyi</a> calls <a href="/wiki/Flow_(psychology)" title="Flow (psychology)">"flow"</a>. "Flow" occurs when an individual forgets about the outside world due to being thoroughly engaged in a process or activity. Csikszentmihalyi suggested that breakthrough innovations tend to occur at the hands of individuals in that state.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>160<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Other research has concluded that a strong internal motivation is a vital ingredient for breakthrough innovation.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-ChristensenJohnson2008&#95;161-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ChristensenJohnson2008-161"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>161<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Flow can be compared to <a href="/wiki/Maria_Montessori" title="Maria Montessori">Maria Montessori</a>'s concept of normalization, a state that includes a child's capacity for joyful and lengthy periods of intense concentration.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>162<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Csikszentmihalyi acknowledged that Montessori's prepared environment offers children opportunities to achieve flow.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>163<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Thus quality and type of early education may influence entrepreneurial capability.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>Research on high-risk settings such as oil platforms, <a href="/wiki/Investment_banking" title="Investment banking">investment banking</a>, medical surgery, aircraft piloting and nuclear-power plants has related distrust to failure avoidance.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>164<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> When non-routine strategies are needed, distrusting persons perform better, while when routine strategies are needed trusting persons perform better. Gudmundsson and Lechner extended this research to entrepreneurial firms.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>165<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> They argued that in entrepreneurial firms the threat of failure is ever-present, resembling non-routine situations in high-risk settings. They found that the firms of distrusting entrepreneurs were more likely to survive than the firms of optimistic or overconfident entrepreneurs. The reasons were that distrusting entrepreneurs would emphasize failure-avoidance through sensible task selection and more analysis. Kets de Vries has pointed out that distrusting entrepreneurs are more alert about their external environment.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>166<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> He concluded that distrusting entrepreneurs are less likely to discount negative events and are more likely to engage control mechanisms. Similarly, Gudmundsson and Lechner found that distrust leads to higher precaution and therefore increases chances of entrepreneurial-firm survival.
</p><p>In recent decades, researchers have examined the social and psychological traits that characterize entrepreneurs, which could potentially help identify those who may become entrepreneurs in the future. Entrepreneurial personality is associated with high self-efficacy, autonomy, innovativeness, internal locus of control, achievement motivation, optimism, and stress tolerance.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>167<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>168<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Research published in 2022 revealed that enterprising tendency is negatively associated with trait victimhood (a persistent tendency to see oneself as a victim) and that among people with lower self-efficacy, having lower trait victimhood predicted more behavioral entrepreneurship (founding at least one business initiative).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>169<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
Researchers Schoon and Duckworth completed a study in 2012 that could potentially help identify who may become an entrepreneur at an early age. They determined that the best measures to identify a young entrepreneur are family and social status, parental role-modelling, entrepreneurial competencies at age 10, academic attainment at age 10, generalized <a href="/wiki/Self-efficacy" title="Self-efficacy">self-efficacy</a>, social skills, entrepreneurial intention and experience of unemployment.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>170<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Strategic_entrepreneurship">Strategic entrepreneurship</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Entrepreneurship&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Strategic entrepreneurship"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Some scholars have constructed an operational definition of a more specific subcategory called "Strategic Entrepreneurship". Closely tied with principles of <a href="/wiki/Strategic_management" title="Strategic management">strategic management</a>, this form of entrepreneurship is "concerned about growth, creating value for customers and subsequently creating wealth for owners".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Hitt,&#95;M.&#95;A.&#95;2011&#95;171-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hitt,_M._A._2011-171"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>171<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> A 2011 article for the Academy of Management provided a three-step, "Input-Process-Output" model of strategic entrepreneurship. The model's three steps entail the collection of different resources, the process of orchestrating them in the necessary manner and the subsequent creation of competitive advantage, value for customers, wealth and other benefits. Through the proper use of strategic management/<a href="/wiki/Strategic_leadership" title="Strategic leadership">leadership</a> techniques and the implementation of risk-bearing entrepreneurial thinking, the strategic entrepreneur is, therefore, able to align resources to create value and wealth.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Hitt,&#95;M.&#95;A.&#95;2011&#95;171-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hitt,_M._A._2011-171"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>171<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Leadership">Leadership</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Entrepreneurship&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Leadership"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Some aspects of <a href="/wiki/Leadership" title="Leadership">leadership</a> within entrepreneurship can be defined as a subset of the leading of any group: a "process of social influence in which one person can enlist the aid and support of others in the accomplishment of a common task"<sup id="cite&#95;ref-172" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-172"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>172<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> in "one who undertakes innovations, finance and business acumen in an effort to transform innovations into economic goods".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>173<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (March 2020)">page&#160;needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="The material near this tag failed verification of its source citation(s). (August 2025)">failed verification</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>This refers to not only the act of entrepreneurship as managing or starting a business, but how one achieves entrepreneurial success  by such social processes, or by <a href="/wiki/Leadership_skills" class="mw-redirect" title="Leadership skills">leadership skills</a>. (Entrepreneurship in itself can be defined somewhat circularly as "the process by which individuals, teams, or organizations identify and pursue entrepreneurial opportunities without being immediately constrained by the resources they currently control".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-ReferenceA&#95;174-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-174"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>174<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (August 2025)">page&#160;needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>) An entrepreneur typically has a mindset that seeks out potential opportunities during uncertain times.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-ReferenceA&#95;174-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-174"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>174<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="Quotation needed from source to verify. (August 2025)">need quotation to verify</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>With the growing global market and increasing technology-use throughout all industries,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2025)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> the core of entrepreneurship and the decision-making has become an ongoing process rather than isolated incidents.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> This becomes <a href="/wiki/Knowledge_management" title="Knowledge management">knowledge management</a>,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> which is "identifying and harnessing intellectual assets"<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2025)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> for organizations to "build on past experiences and create new mechanisms for exchanging and creating knowledge".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>175<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This belief<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Avoid_weasel_words" class="mw-redirect" title="Wikipedia:Avoid weasel words"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too vague attribution or weasel words. (March 2020)">which?</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> draws upon a <a href="/wiki/Leader" class="mw-redirect" title="Leader">leader</a>'s past experiences that may prove useful. It is a common mantra that one should learn from past mistakes, so leaders should take advantage of their failures for their benefit.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> This is how one may take experiences as a leader for the use in the core of entrepreneurship decision-making.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Global_leadership">Global leadership</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Entrepreneurship&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Global leadership"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The majority of scholarly research done on these topics has taken place in North America.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-176" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-176"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>176<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Words like "leadership" and "entrepreneurship" do not always translate well into other cultures and languages. For example, in North America a leader is often thought of as charismatic, but German culture frowns on such charisma due to the charisma of <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Party" title="Nazi Party">Nazi</a> leader <a href="/wiki/Adolf_Hitler" title="Adolf Hitler">Adolf Hitler</a> (1889–1945). Other cultures, as in some European countries, view the term "leader" negatively, like the French.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-177" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-177"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>177<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="Quotation needed from source to verify. (March 2020)">need quotation to verify</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
The participative leadership style that is prevalent in the United States is considered disrespectful in many other parts of the world due to the differences in power distance.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>178<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Many Asian and Middle Eastern countries do not have "open door" policies for subordinates, who would never informally approach their managers/bosses. For countries like that, an authoritarian approach to management and leadership is more customary.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>Despite cultural differences, the successes and failures of entrepreneurs can be traced to how leaders adapt to local conditions.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-179" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-179"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>179<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Within the increasingly global business environment a successful leader must be able to adapt and have insight into other cultures. To respond to the environment, corporate visions are becoming transnational in nature, to enable the organization to operate in or provide services/goods for other cultures.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-180" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-180"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>180<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Entrepreneurship_training_and_education">Entrepreneurship training and education</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Entrepreneurship&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Entrepreneurship training and education"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Michelacci and Schivardi are a pair of researchers who believe that identifying and comparing the relationships between an entrepreneur's earnings and education level would determine the rate and level of success. Their study focused on two education levels, college degree and post-graduate degree. While Michelacci and Schivardi do not specifically determine characteristics or traits for successful entrepreneurs, they do believe that there is a direct relationship between education and success, noting that having a college knowledge does contribute to advancement in the workforce.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Michelacci&#95;and&#95;Schivardi&#95;181-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Michelacci_and_Schivardi-181"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>181<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>However, mentorship programs also provide valuable support and guidance for aspiring entrepreneurs. For example, mentorship program connects experienced entrepreneurs with individuals looking to start their own businesses.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-182" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-182"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>182<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Michelacci and Schivardi state there has been a rise in the number of self-employed people with a baccalaureate degree. However, their findings also show that those who are self-employed and possess a graduate degree has remained consistent throughout time at about 33 percent. They briefly mention those famous entrepreneurs like <a href="/wiki/Steve_Jobs" title="Steve Jobs">Steve Jobs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mark_Zuckerberg" title="Mark Zuckerberg">Mark Zuckerberg</a> who were college dropouts, but they don't consider these cases to be exceptional as many entrepreneurs view formal education as costly due to the <a href="/wiki/Opportunity_cost" title="Opportunity cost">time that needs to be spent on it</a>.  Michelacci and Schivardi believe that for an individual to reach the full success they need to have education beyond high school. Their research shows that the higher the education level the greater the success. The reason is that college gives people additional skills that can be used within their business and to operate on a higher level than someone who only "runs" it.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Michelacci&#95;and&#95;Schivardi&#95;181-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Michelacci_and_Schivardi-181"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>181<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>According to a 2025 study, almost 75% of male entrepreneurs start a firm in an industry in the same or related industry as that of their father.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:1&#95;183-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-183"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>183<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Such ventures tend to be more successful, as the sons obtain industry knowledge through informal interactions with their fathers.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:1&#95;183-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-183"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>183<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> 
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Resources_and_financing">Resources and financing</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Entrepreneurship&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Resources and financing"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Entrepreneurial_resources">Entrepreneurial resources</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Entrepreneurship&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Entrepreneurial resources"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>An entrepreneurial resource is any company-owned <a href="/wiki/Asset" title="Asset">asset</a> that has <a href="/wiki/Economic_value" class="mw-redirect" title="Economic value">economic value</a> creating capabilities. Economic value creating both tangible and intangible sources are considered as entrepreneurial resources. Their economic value is generating activities or services through mobilization by entrepreneurs.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-184" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-184"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>184<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Entrepreneurial resources can be divided into two fundamental categories: tangible and intangible resources.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-185" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-185"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>185<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Tangible resources are material sources such as equipment, building, furniture, land, vehicle, machinery, stock, cash, bond and inventory that has a physical form and can be quantified. On the contrary, intangible resources are nonphysical or more challenging to identify and evaluate, and they possess more value creating capacity such as human resources including skills and experience in a particular field, organizational structure of the company, brand name, reputation, entrepreneurial networks that contribute to promotion and financial support, <a href="/wiki/Know-how" class="mw-redirect" title="Know-how">know-how</a>, intellectual property including both <a href="/wiki/Copyright" title="Copyright">copyrights</a>, trademarks and patents.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-186" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-186"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>186<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-187" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-187"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>187<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Bootstrapping">Bootstrapping</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Entrepreneurship&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Bootstrapping"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Contextual_background">Contextual background</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Entrepreneurship&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Contextual background"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>At least early on, entrepreneurs often "bootstrap-finance" their start-up rather than seeking external investors from the start. One of the reasons that some entrepreneurs prefer to "bootstrap" is that obtaining <a href="/wiki/Equity_financing" class="mw-redirect" title="Equity financing">equity financing</a> requires the entrepreneur to provide ownership shares to the investors. If the start-up becomes successful later on, these early equity financing deals could provide a windfall for the investors and a huge loss for the entrepreneur. If investors have a significant stake in the company, they may as well be able to exert influence on company strategy, chief executive officer (CEO) choice and other important decisions. This is often problematic since the investor and the founder might have different incentives regarding the long-term goal of the company. An investor will generally aim for a profitable exit and therefore promotes a high-valuation sale of the company or IPO to sell their shares. Whereas the entrepreneur might have philanthropic intentions as their main driving force. Soft values like this might not go well with the short-term pressure on yearly and quarterly profits that publicly traded companies often experience from their owners.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-188" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-188"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>188<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Common_definition">Common definition</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Entrepreneurship&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Common definition"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>One consensus definition of bootstrapping sees it as "a collection of methods used to minimize the amount of outside debt and equity financing needed from banks and investors".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-189" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-189"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>189<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Related_methodologies">Related methodologies</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Entrepreneurship&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Related methodologies"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Bootstrapping methods include:<sup id="cite&#95;ref-NarayananO&#39;Connor2010&#95;190-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NarayananO&#39;Connor2010-190"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>190<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>Owner financing, including savings, personal loans and credit card debt</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Working_capital" title="Working capital">Working capital</a> management that minimizes <a href="/wiki/Accounts_receivable" title="Accounts receivable">accounts receivable</a></li>
<li>Joint use, such as reducing <a href="/wiki/Overhead_(business)" title="Overhead (business)">overhead</a> by <a href="/wiki/Coworking" title="Coworking">coworking</a> or using <a href="/wiki/Independent_contractor" class="mw-redirect" title="Independent contractor">independent contractors</a></li>
<li>Increasing <a href="/wiki/Accounts_payable" title="Accounts payable">accounts payable</a> by delaying payment, or leasing rather than buying equipment</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lean_manufacturing" title="Lean manufacturing">Lean manufacturing</a> strategies such as minimizing inventory and <a href="/wiki/Lean_startup" title="Lean startup">lean startup</a> to reduce product development costs</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Government_subsidy" class="mw-redirect" title="Government subsidy">Subsidy finance</a></li></ul>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Additional_financing">Additional financing</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Entrepreneurship&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Additional financing"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1305433154" /><table class="box-Unreferenced&#95;section plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Unreferenced" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div class="mbox-image-div"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="icon" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/60px-Question_book-new.svg.png" decoding="async" width="50" height="39" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/120px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="399" /></a></span></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>does not <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources">cite</a> any <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">sources</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a href="/wiki/Special:EditPage/Entrepreneurship" title="Special:EditPage/Entrepreneurship">improve this section</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Referencing_for_beginners" title="Help:Referencing for beginners">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability#Burden_of_evidence" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">removed</a>.</span>  <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">August 2021</span>)</i></span><span class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<small><a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this message</a></small>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Many businesses need more capital than can be provided by the owners themselves. In this case, a range of options is available including a wide variety of <a href="/wiki/Private_equity" title="Private equity">private</a> and public equity, <a href="/wiki/Debt" title="Debt">debt</a> and <a href="/wiki/Grant_(money)" title="Grant (money)">grants</a>. Private equity options include:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Startup_accelerator" title="Startup accelerator">Start-up accelerators</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Angel_investor" title="Angel investor">Angel investors</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Venture_capital" title="Venture capital">Venture capital</a> investors</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Equity_crowdfunding" title="Equity crowdfunding">Equity crowdfunding</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Hedge_fund" title="Hedge fund">Hedge funds</a></li></ul>
<p>Debt options open to entrepreneurs include:
</p>
<ul><li>Loans from banks, specialized financial companies (such as <a href="/wiki/Payment_card_industry" title="Payment card industry">credit card companies</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Economic_development" title="Economic development">economic development</a> organizations</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Line_of_credit" title="Line of credit">Line of credit</a> also from banks and specialized financial companies</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Microcredit" title="Microcredit">Microcredit</a> also known as microloans</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Merchant_cash_advance" title="Merchant cash advance">Merchant cash advance</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Revenue-based_financing" title="Revenue-based financing">Revenue-based financing</a></li></ul>
<p>Grant options open to entrepreneurs include:
</p>
<ul><li>Equity-free accelerators</li>
<li>Business plan/business pitch competitions for college entrepreneurs and others</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Small_Business_Innovation_Research" title="Small Business Innovation Research">Small Business Innovation Research</a> grants from the U.S. government</li></ul>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Effect_of_taxes">Effect of taxes</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Entrepreneurship&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Effect of taxes"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Entrepreneurs are faced with <a href="/wiki/Liquidity_constraint" title="Liquidity constraint">liquidity constraints</a> and often lack the necessary <a href="/wiki/Credit_(finance)" class="mw-redirect" title="Credit (finance)">credit</a> needed to borrow large amounts of money to finance their venture.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:2&#95;191-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-191"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>191<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Because of this, many studies have been done on the effects of taxes on entrepreneurs. The studies fall into two camps: the first camp finds that taxes help and the second argues that taxes hurt entrepreneurship.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>
Cesaire Assah Meh found that corporate taxes create an incentive to become an entrepreneur to avoid double taxation.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:2&#95;191-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-191"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>191<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Donald Bruce and John Deskins found literature suggesting that a higher corporate tax rate may reduce a state's share of entrepreneurs.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:3&#95;192-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-192"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>192<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> They also found that states with an inheritance or <a href="/wiki/Estate_tax" class="mw-redirect" title="Estate tax">estate tax</a> tend to have lower entrepreneurship rates when using a tax-based measure.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:3&#95;192-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-192"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>192<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> However, another study found that states with a more <a href="/wiki/Progressive_taxation" class="mw-redirect" title="Progressive taxation">progressive personal income tax</a> have a higher percentage of sole proprietors in their workforce.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-193" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-193"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>193<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Ultimately, many studies find that the effect of taxes on the probability of becoming an entrepreneur is small. Donald Bruce and Mohammed Mohsin found that it would take a 50 percentage point drop in the top tax rate to produce a one percent change in entrepreneurial activity.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-194" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-194"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>194<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></p><figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Computer_industry_entrepreneur_workshop.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Computer_industry_entrepreneur_workshop.jpg/250px-Computer_industry_entrepreneur_workshop.jpg" decoding="async" width="230" height="153" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Computer_industry_entrepreneur_workshop.jpg/500px-Computer_industry_entrepreneur_workshop.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="3000" data-file-height="1997" /></a><figcaption><a href="/wiki/Dell" title="Dell">Dell</a> Women's Entrepreneur <a href="/wiki/Business_networking" title="Business networking">Network</a> event in New York City</figcaption></figure>
<ul><li class="mw-empty-elt"></li></ul>

Oxford University Press. p.&#160;40. <a href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0195170313" title="Special:BookSources/978-0195170313"><bdi>978-0195170313</bdi></a>.</cite><span title="ctx&#95;ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft&#95;val&#95;fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=The+Origin+and+Evolution+of+New+Businesses&amp;rft.pages=40&amp;rft.pub=Oxford+University+Press&amp;rft.date=1999&amp;rft.isbn=978-0195170313&amp;rft.aulast=Bhide&amp;rft.aufirst=Amar&amp;rft&#95;id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.bhide.net%2Fbooks%2FOrigin%2520and%2520Evolution%2Fpart1.pdf&amp;rfr&#95;id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AEntrepreneurship" class="Z3988"></span></span>

<li id="cite&#95;note-189"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-189">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1333433106" /><cite id="CITEREFEbbenaJohnson2006" class="citation journal cs1">Ebbena, Jay; Johnson, Alec (2006). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://archive.org/details/sim_journal-of-business-venturing_2006-11_21_6/page/851">"Bootstrapping in small firms: An empirical analysis of change over time"</a>. <i>Journal of Business Venturing</i>. <b>21</b> (6) (published November 2006): <span class="nowrap">851–</span>865. <a href="/wiki/Doi_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="Doi (identifier)">doi</a>:<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.1016%2Fj.jbusvent.2005.06.007">10.1016/j.jbusvent.2005.06.007</a>. <q>Bootstrapping has taken on many definitions in the literature, but there has been some recent consensus that it is a collection of methods used to minimize the amount of outside debt and equity financing needed from banks and investors (Winborg and Landstrom, 2001 and Harrison and Mason, 1997).</q></cite><span title="ctx&#95;ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft&#95;val&#95;fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Journal+of+Business+Venturing&amp;rft.atitle=Bootstrapping+in+small+firms%3A+An+empirical+analysis+of+change+over+time&amp;rft.volume=21&amp;rft.issue=6&amp;rft.pages=851-865&amp;rft.date=2006&amp;rft&#95;id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1016%2Fj.jbusvent.2005.06.007&amp;rft.aulast=Ebbena&amp;rft.aufirst=Jay&amp;rft.au=Johnson%2C+Alec&amp;rft&#95;id=https%3A%2F%2Farchive.org%2Fdetails%2Fsim&#95;journal-of-business-venturing&#95;2006-11&#95;21&#95;6%2Fpage%2F851&amp;rfr&#95;id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AEntrepreneurship" class="Z3988"></span></span>
